In contrast to the first, logical part of Hegel's system, the second, [real-philosophical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Realphilosophie "Realphilosophie") part – the philosophy of nature and of spirit – is an ongoing project with respect to its historical content, which continues to change and develop.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis200529–31_162-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis200529%E2%80%9331-162">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel199554–55_163-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel199554%E2%80%9355-163">[145]</a></sup> For instance, although Hegel regards "the basic structure" of the philosophy of nature as complete, he was "aware that science is not 'finished' and will continue to make new discoveries".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagee2011156_164-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagee2011156-164">[146]</a></sup> Philosophy is, as Hegel puts it, "_its own time comprehended in thoughts_."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1991a21_165-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1991a21-165">[147]</a></sup>

He expands upon this definition:

> A further word on the subject of _issuing instructions_ on how the world ought to be: philosophy, at any rate, always comes too late to perform this function. As the _thought_ of the world, it appears only at a time when actuality has gone through its formative process and attained its completed state \[_sich fertig gemacht_\]. This lesson of the concept is necessarily also apparent from history, namely that it is only when actuality \[_Wirklichkeit_\] has reached maturity that the ideal appears opposite the real and reconstructs this real world, which it has grasped in its substance, in the shape of an intellectual realm. When philosophy paints its gray in gray, a shape of life has grown old, and it cannot be rejuvenated, but only recognized, by the gray in gray of philosophy; the owl of [Minerva](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minerva "Minerva") begins its flight only with the onset of dusk.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1991a23_166-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1991a23-166">[148]</a></sup>

This frequently has been read as an expression of the impotence of philosophy, political or otherwise, and a rationalization of the status quo.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood1991viii–ix_167-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood1991viii%E2%80%93ix-167">[149]</a></sup> [Allegra de Laurentiis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allegra_de_Laurentiis "Allegra de Laurentiis"), however, points out that the German expression "_sich fertig machen_", translated as "getting ready" or "to get ready", does not only imply completion, but also preparedness. This additional meaning is important because it better reflects Hegel's [Aristotelian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotelianism "Aristotelianism") concept of [actuality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potentiality_and_actuality "Potentiality and actuality"). He characterizes actuality as being-at-work-staying-itself that can never be once-and-for-all completed or finished.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis200529_168-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis200529-168">[150]</a></sup>

Hegel describes the relationship between the logical and the real-philosophical parts of his system in this way: "If philosophy does not stand above its time in content, it does so in [form](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Substantial_form "Substantial form"), because, as the thought and knowledge of that which is the substantial spirit of its time, it makes that spirit its object."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel199554_169-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel199554-169">[151]</a></sup>

This is to say that what makes the philosophy of the real [_scientific_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wissenschaft "Wissenschaft") in Hegel's technical sense is the systematically coherent logical form it uncovers in its natural-historical material – and so also displays in its presentation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInwood1992265–68_170-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInwood1992265%E2%80%9368-170">[152]</a></sup>

### _The Philosophy of Nature_\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: The Philosophy of Nature")\]

The [philosophy of nature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naturphilosophie "Naturphilosophie") organizes the contingent material of the natural sciences systematically. As part of the [philosophy of the real](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Realphilosophie "Realphilosophie"), in no way does it presume to "tell nature what it must be like."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005108–09_171-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005108%E2%80%9309-171">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurbidge2006a_172-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurbidge2006a-172">[154]</a></sup> Historically, various interpreters have questioned Hegel's understanding of the natural sciences of his time. However, this claim has been largely refuted by recent scholarship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestphal2008281–310_173-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestphal2008281%E2%80%93310-173">[155]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagee2011155_174-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagee2011155-174">[156]</a></sup>

One of the very few ways in which the philosophy of nature might correct claims made by the natural sciences themselves is to combat reductive explanations; that is to discredit accounts employing categories not adequate to the complexity of the phenomena they purport to explain, as for instance, attempting to explain life in strictly chemical terms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulgate2005199_175-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulgate2005199-175">[157]</a></sup>

Although Hegel and other _Naturphilosophen_ aim to revive a teleological understanding of nature, they argue that their strictly _internal_ or _immanent_ concept of teleology is "limited to the ends observable within nature itself." Hence, they claim, it does not violate the Kantian critique.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005101_176-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005101-176">[158]</a></sup> Even more strongly, Hegel and Schelling claim that Kant's restriction of teleology to regulative status effectively undermines his own critical project of explaining the possibility of knowledge. Their argument is that "only under the assumption that _there is_ an organism is it possible to explain the _actual interaction_ between the subjective and the objective, the ideal and the real." Hence the organism must be acknowledged to have constitutive status.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005106–07_177-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005106%E2%80%9307-177">[159]</a></sup>

Introducing Hegel's philosophy of nature for a 21st-century audience, [Dieter Wandschneider](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dieter_Wandschneider&action=edit&redlink=1 "Dieter Wandschneider (page does not exist)") \[[de](https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dieter_Wandschneider "de:Dieter Wandschneider")\] observes that "contemporary philosophy of science" has lost sight of "the ontological issue at stake, namely, the question of an intrinsically lawful nature": "Consider, for example, the problem of what constitutes a law of nature. This problem is central to our understanding of nature. Yet philosophy of science has not provided a definitive response to it up to now. Nor can we expect to have such an answer from that quarter in future."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWandschneider2013343_178-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWandschneider2013343-178">[160]</a></sup> It is back to Hegel that Wandschneider would direct [philosophers of science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy_of_science "Philosophy of science") for guidance in the [philosophy of nature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naturphilosophie "Naturphilosophie").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWandschneider2013_179-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWandschneider2013-179">[161]</a></sup>

Recent scholars have also argued that Hegel's approach to the philosophy of nature provides valuable resources for theorizing and confronting recent environmental challenges entirely unforeseen by Hegel. These philosophers point to such aspects of his philosophy as its distinctive metaphysical grounding and the continuity of its conception of the nature-spirit relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone2005_180-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone2005-180">[162]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBernstein2023_181-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBernstein2023-181">[163]</a></sup>

### _The Philosophy of Spirit_\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: The Philosophy of Spirit")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/John_Collier_-_Priestess_of_Delphi.jpg/170px-John_Collier_-_Priestess_of_Delphi.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:John_Collier_-_Priestess_of_Delphi.jpg)

_Priestess of Delphi_ (1891) by [John Collier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Collier_(Pre-Raphaelite_painter) "John Collier (Pre-Raphaelite painter)"). The Delphic imperative to "know thyself" governs Hegel's entire philosophy of spirit.

The German _Geist_ has a wide range of meanings.<sup id="cite_ref-182"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-182">[164]</a></sup> In its most general Hegelian sense, however, "_Geist_ denotes the human mind and its products, in contrast to nature and also the logical idea."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInwood1992275_183-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInwood1992275-183">[165]</a></sup> (Some older translations render it as "mind," rather than "spirit."<sup id="cite_ref-185"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-185">[s]</a></sup>)

As is especially evident in the Anthropology, Hegel's concept of spirit is an appropriation and transformation of the self-referential Aristotelian concept of _[energeia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Energeia "Energeia")_.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrarin20077–8_186-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrarin20077%E2%80%938-186">[167]</a></sup> Spirit is not something above or otherwise external to nature. It is "the highest organization and development" of nature's powers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005112_187-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005112-187">[168]</a></sup>

According to Hegel, "the _essence_ of spirit is _freedom_."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1991b§382_188-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1991b%C2%A7382-188">[169]</a></sup> The _Encyclopedia_ Philosophy of Spirit charts the progressively determinate stages of this freedom until spirit fulfills the [Delphic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pythia "Pythia") imperative with which Hegel begins: "_Know thyself_."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel2010b§377_189-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel2010b%C2%A7377-189">[170]</a></sup>

As becomes clear, Hegel's concept of freedom is not (or not merely) the capacity for arbitrary choice, but has as its "core notion" that "something, especially a person, is free if and only if, it is independent and self-determining, not determined by or dependent upon something other than itself."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInwood1992110_190-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInwood1992110-190">[171]</a></sup> It is, in other words, (at least predominantly, dialectically) an account of what [Isaiah Berlin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaiah_Berlin "Isaiah Berlin") would later term [positive liberty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positive_liberty "Positive liberty").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarter2022_191-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarter2022-191">[172]</a></sup>

#### Subjective spirit\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Subjective spirit")\]

Standing at the transition from nature to spirit, the role of the Philosophy of Subjective Spirit is to analyze "the elements necessary for or presupposed by such relations \[of objective spirit\], namely, the structures characteristic of and necessary to the individual rational agent." It does this by elaborating "the fundamental nature of the biological/spiritual human individual along with the cognitive and the practical prerequisites of human social interaction."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEdeVries2013133_192-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEdeVries2013133-192">[173]</a></sup>

This section, particularly its first part, contains various comments that were commonplace in Hegel's day and can now be recognized as openly racist, such as unfounded claims about the "naturally" lower intellectual and emotional development of Black people. In his perspective, these racial differences are related to _climate_: according to Hegel, it is not racial characteristics, but the climactic conditions in which a people lives that variously limit or enable its capacity for free self-determination. He believes that race is not destiny: any group could, in principle, improve and transform its condition by migrating to friendlier climes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFritzman2014103–04_193-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFritzman2014103%E2%80%9304-193">[174]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-194">[t]</a></sup>

Hegel divides his philosophy of the subjective spirit into three parts: anthropology, phenomenology, and psychology. Anthropology "deals with 'soul', which is spirit still mired in nature: all that within us which precedes our self-conscious mind or intellect." In the section "Phenomenology", Hegel examines the relation between consciousness and its object and the emergence of intersubjective rationality. Psychology "deals with a great deal that would be categorized as epistemology (or 'theory of knowledge') today. Hegel discusses, among other things, the nature of attention, memory, imagination and judgement."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagee2011p._235,_caps_modified_195-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagee2011p._235,_caps_modified-195">[175]</a></sup>

Throughout this section, but especially in the Anthropology, Hegel appropriates and develops [Aristotle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotle "Aristotle")'s [hylomorphic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hylomorphic "Hylomorphic") approach to what is today theorized as the [mind–body problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_problem "Mind–body problem"): "The solution to the mind–body problem \[according to this theory\] hinges upon recognizing that mind does not act upon the body as cause of effects but rather acts upon itself as an embodied living subjectivity. As such, mind develops itself, progressively attaining more and more of a self-determined character."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDien_Winfield2011236_196-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDien_Winfield2011236-196">[176]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis2021_197-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis2021-197">[177]</a></sup>

Its final section, Free Spirit, develops the concept of "free will," which is foundational for Hegel's philosophy of right.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeperzak2001174_198-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeperzak2001174-198">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1991a§4_199-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1991a%C2%A74-199">[179]</a></sup>

#### Objective spirit\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Objective spirit")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/King_Frederick_William_III_of_Prussia.jpg/180px-King_Frederick_William_III_of_Prussia.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:King_Frederick_William_III_of_Prussia.jpg)

[King Frederick William III of Prussia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/King_Frederick_William_III_of_Prussia "King Frederick William III of Prussia") (1797—1840) stifled the political reforms for which Hegel had hoped and advocated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood1991ix–x_200-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood1991ix%E2%80%93x-200">[180]</a></sup>

In the broadest terms, Hegel's philosophy of objective spirit "is his social philosophy, his philosophy of how the human spirit objectifies itself in its social and historical activities and productions."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestphal2013157_201-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestphal2013157-201">[181]</a></sup> Or, put differently, it is an account of the institutionalization of freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPippin2008b_202-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPippin2008b-202">[182]</a></sup> Besier declares this a rare instance of unanimity in Hegel scholarship: "all scholars agree there is no more important concept in Hegel's political theory than freedom." This is because it is the foundation of right, the essence of spirit, and the _telos_ of history.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005197_203-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005197-203">[183]</a></sup>

This part of Hegel's philosophy is presented first in his 1817 _[Encyclopedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_the_Philosophical_Sciences "Encyclopedia of the Philosophical Sciences")_ (revised 1827 and 1830) and then at greater length in the 1821 _[Elements of the Philosophy of Right, or Natural Law and Political Science in Outline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elements_of_the_Philosophy_of_Right "Elements of the Philosophy of Right")_ (like the _Encyclopedia_, intended as a textbook), upon which he also frequently lectured. Its final part, the philosophy of world history, was additionally elaborated in [Hegel's lectures on the subject](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lectures_on_the_Philosophy_of_History "Lectures on the Philosophy of History").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinkard2000375_204-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinkard2000375-204">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagee2011186_205-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagee2011186-205">[185]</a></sup>

Hegel's _Elements of the Philosophy of Right_ has been controversial from the date of its original publication.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood1991viii–x_206-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood1991viii%E2%80%93x-206">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinkard2000457–61_207-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinkard2000457%E2%80%9361-207">[187]</a></sup> It is not, however, a straightforward defense of the autocratic Prussian state, as some have alleged, but is rather a defense of "Prussia as it was to have become under \[proposed\] reform administrations."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood1991x_208-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood1991x-208">[188]</a></sup>

The German [_Recht_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Translating_%22law%22_to_other_European_languages "Translating "law" to other European languages") in Hegel's title does not have a direct English equivalent (though it does correspond to the Latin _ius_ and the French _droit_). As a first approximation, Michael Inwood distinguishes three senses:

-   a right, claim or title
-   justice (as in, e.g., 'to administer justice'...but not justice as a virtue...)
-   'the law' as a principle, or 'the laws' collectively.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInwood1992259_209-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInwood1992259-209">[189]</a></sup>

Beiser observes that Hegel's theory is "his attempt to rehabilitate the [natural law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_law "Natural law") tradition while taking into account the criticisms of the historical school." He adds that "without a sound interpretation of Hegel's theory of natural law, we have very little understanding of the very foundation of his social and political thought."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser200813–14_210-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser200813%E2%80%9314-210">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-214">[u]</a></sup> Consistent with Beiser's position, [Adriaan T. Peperzak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adriaan_Theodoor_Peperzak "Adriaan Theodoor Peperzak") documents Hegel's arguments against [social contract theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_contract_theory "Social contract theory") and stresses the metaphysical foundations of Hegel's philosophy of right.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeperzak2001_215-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeperzak2001-215">[194]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-217">[v]</a></sup>

Observing that "analyzing the structure of Hegel's argument in the _Philosophy of Right_ shows that achieving political autonomy is fundamental to Hegel's analysis of the state and government," Kenneth R. Westphal provides this brief outline:

-   "'Abstract Right,' treats principles governing property, its transfer, and wrongs against property."
-   "'Morality,' treats the rights of moral subjects, responsibility for one's actions, and [a priori](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_priori "A priori") theories of right."
-   "'[Ethical Life' (_Sittlichkeit_)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sittlichkeit "Sittlichkeit"), analyzes the principles and institutions governing central aspects of rational social life, including the family, [civil society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civil_society "Civil society"), and the [state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nation_state "Nation state") as a whole, including the government."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestphal1993246_218-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestphal1993246-218">[196]</a></sup>

Hegel describes the state of his time, a [constitutional monarchy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy "Constitutional monarchy"), as rationally embodying three cooperative and mutually inclusive elements. These elements are "democracy (rule of the many, who are involved in legislation), [aristocracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristocracy "Aristocracy") (rule of the few, who apply, concretize, and execute the laws), and monarchy (rule of the one, who heads and encompasses all power)."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeperzak2001523_219-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeperzak2001523-219">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1991a§286R_220-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1991a%C2%A7286R-220">[198]</a></sup> It is what Aristotle called a "mixed" form of government, which is designed to include what is best of each of the three classical forms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005252_221-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005252-221">[199]</a></sup> The division of powers "prevents an single power from dominating others."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005253_222-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005253-222">[200]</a></sup> Hegel is particularly concerned to bind the monarch to the constitution, limiting his authority so that he can do little more than to declare of what his ministers have already decided that it is to be so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005254–55_223-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005254%E2%80%9355-223">[201]</a></sup>

The relation of Hegel's philosophy of right to modern liberalism is complex. He sees liberalism as a valuable and characteristic expression of the modern world. However, it carries the danger within itself to undermine its own values. This self-destructive tendency may be avoided by measuring "the subjective goals of individuals by a larger objective and collective good." Moral values, then, have only a "limited place in the total scheme of things."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood1991xi_224-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood1991xi-224">[202]</a></sup> Yet, although it is not without reason that Hegel is widely regarded as a major proponent of what [Isaiah Berlin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaiah_Berlin "Isaiah Berlin") would later term [positive liberty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positive_liberty "Positive liberty"), he was just as "unwavering and unequivocal" in his defense of [negative liberty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negative_liberty "Negative liberty").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005202–05_225-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005202%E2%80%9305-225">[203]</a></sup>

If Hegel's ideal sovereign is much weaker than was typical in monarchies his time, so too is his democratic element much weaker than is typical in democracies of modern times. Although he insists upon the importance of public participation, Hegel severely limits suffrage and follows the English [bicameral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bicameralism "Bicameralism") model, in which only members of the lower house, that of commoners and [bourgeoisie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bourgeoisie "Bourgeoisie"), are elected officials. Nobles in the upper house, like the monarch, inherit their positions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005254–58_226-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005254%E2%80%9358-226">[204]</a></sup>

The final part of the Philosophy of Objective Spirit is entitled "World History." In this section, Hegel argues that "this immanent principle \[the [Stoic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stoicism "Stoicism") _[logos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logos "Logos")_\] produces with logical inevitability an expansion of the species' capacities for self determination ('freedom') and a deepening of its self understanding ('self-knowing')."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis2010207_227-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis2010207-227">[205]</a></sup> In Hegel's own words: "World history is progress in the consciousness of freedom – a progress that we must comprehend conceptually."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis2010207_(quoting_Hegel,_her_translation)_228-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis2010207_(quoting_Hegel,_her_translation)-228">[206]</a></sup>

_(See also: [Legacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#Criticism_and_legacy), below, for further discussion of the complex legacy of Hegel's social and political philosophy.)_

#### Absolute spirit\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: Absolute spirit")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Friedrich_Hegel_mit_Studenten_Lithographie_F_Kugler.jpg/220px-Friedrich_Hegel_mit_Studenten_Lithographie_F_Kugler.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Friedrich_Hegel_mit_Studenten_Lithographie_F_Kugler.jpg)

Hegel with his Berlin students  
(1828 sketch by [F. T. Kugler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franz_Theodor_Kugler "Franz Theodor Kugler"))

Hegel's use of the term "absolute" is easily misunderstood. Inwood, however, clarifies: derived from the Latin _absolutus_, it means "not dependent on, conditional on, relative to or restricted by anything else; self-contained, perfect, complete."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInwood199227_229-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInwood199227-229">[207]</a></sup> For Hegel, this means that absolute knowing can only denote "an 'absolute relation' in which the ground of experience and the experiencing agent are one and the same: the object known is explicitly the subject who knows."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis2009249_230-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis2009249-230">[208]</a></sup> That is, the only "thing" (which is really an activity) that is truly absolute is that which is entirely self-conditioned, and according to Hegel, this only occurs when spirit takes itself up as its own object. The final section of his Philosophy of Spirit presents the three modes of such absolute knowing: art, religion, and philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-232"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-232">[w]</a></sup>

It is with reference to different modalities of consciousness – [intuition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anschauung "Anschauung"), representation, and comprehending thinking – that Hegel distinguishes the three modes of absolute knowing.<sup id="cite_ref-233"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-233">[x]</a></sup> [Frederick Beiser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frederick_C._Beiser "Frederick C. Beiser") summarizes: "art, religion and philosophy all have the same object, the absolute or truth itself; but they consist in different forms of knowledge of it. Art presents the absolute in the form of immediate intuition (_Anschauung_); religion presents it in the form of representation (_Vorstellung_); and philosophy presents it in the form of concepts (_Begriffe_)."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005288_234-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005288-234">[210]</a></sup>

[Rüdiger Bubner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/R%C3%BCdiger_Bubner "Rüdiger Bubner") additionally clarifies that the increase in conceptual transparency according to which these spheres are systematically ordered is not hierarchical in any evaluative sense.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBubner2007296_235-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBubner2007296-235">[211]</a></sup>

Although Hegel's discussion of absolute spirit in the _Encyclopedia_ is quite brief, he develops his account at length in [lectures on the philosophy of fine art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lectures_on_Aesthetics "Lectures on Aesthetics"), [the philosophy of religion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lectures_on_the_Philosophy_of_Religion "Lectures on the Philosophy of Religion"), and [the history of philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lectures_on_the_History_of_Philosophy "Lectures on the History of Philosophy").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagee2011186_205-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagee2011186-205">[185]</a></sup>

## Philosophy of art\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Philosophy of art")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/NAMA_Ath%C3%A9na_Varvakeion.jpg/170px-NAMA_Ath%C3%A9na_Varvakeion.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NAMA_Ath%C3%A9na_Varvakeion.jpg)

The ancient Athenian, according to Hegel, apprehends the meaning of Athena Parthenos directly as his own rational essence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1975a427_236-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1975a427-236">[212]</a></sup> (The [Varvakeion Athena](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Varvakeion_Athena "Varvakeion Athena"), [National Archaeological Museum of Athens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Archaeological_Museum_of_Athens "National Archaeological Museum of Athens"))

In the _Phenomenology_, and even in the 1817 edition of the _Encyclopedia_, Hegel discusses art only as it figures in what he terms the "Art-Religion" of the ancient Greeks. In 1818, however, Hegel begins lecturing on the philosophy of art as an explicitly autonomous domain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBubner2007296_235-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBubner2007296-235">[211]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoland199317_237-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoland199317-237">[213]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-238">[y]</a></sup>

Although [H. G. Hotho](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Gustav_Hotho "Heinrich Gustav Hotho") titled his edition of the Lectures _Vorlesungen über die Ästhetik_ \[_Lectures on Aesthetics_\], Hegel directly states that his topic is not "the spacious realm of the beautiful," but "art, or, rather, fine art."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1975a1_239-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1975a1-239">[214]</a></sup> He doubles down on this in the next paragraph by explicitly distinguishing his project from the broader philosophical projects pursued under the heading of "aesthetics" by [Christian Wolff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Wolff_(philosopher) "Christian Wolff (philosopher)") and [Alexander Gottlieb Baumgarten](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Gottlieb_Baumgarten "Alexander Gottlieb Baumgarten").<sup id="cite_ref-240"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-240">[z]</a></sup>

Some critics – most canonically, [Benedetto Croce](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benedetto_Croce "Benedetto Croce"), in 1907<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroce1915130_241-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroce1915130-241">[215]</a></sup> – have attributed to Hegel some form of the thesis that art is "dead." Hegel, however, never said any such thing, nor can such a view be plausibly attributed to him.<sup id="cite_ref-242"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-242">[aa]</a></sup> Indeed, one commentator places that debate in perspective with the observation that Hegel's claim that "art no longer serves our highest aims" is "radical not for the suggestion that art now fails to do so but for the suggestion that it ever did."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutter201024_243-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutter201024-243">[216]</a></sup>

Hegel's detailed and systematic treatment of the various arts over such a great span has even led [Ernst Gombrich](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernst_Gombrich "Ernst Gombrich") to present Hegel as "the father of art history." Indeed, until recently,<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (April 2024)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> Hegel's _Lectures_ were largely ignored by philosophers and received most of their attention from literary critics and art historians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005282_244-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005282-244">[217]</a></sup>

The more narrowly conceptual project of the philosophy of art, however, is to articulate and defend "the _autonomy_ of art, making possible an account of the _special individuality_ distinguishing works of aesthetic worth."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDien_Winfield19959,_emphases_added_245-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDien_Winfield19959,_emphases_added-245">[218]</a></sup>

According to Hegel, "'artistic beauty reveals absolute truth through perception.'<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1975a111_246-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1975a111-246">[219]</a></sup> He holds that the best art conveys metaphysical knowledge by revealing, through sense perception, what is unconditionally true," that is, "what his metaphysical theory affirms to be unconditional or absolute."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks1993349–50_247-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks1993349%E2%80%9350-247">[220]</a></sup> So, while Hegel "ennobles art insofar as it conveys metaphysical knowledge," "he tempers his assessment in view of his belief that art's sensory media can never adequately convey what completely transcends the contingency of sensation."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks1993350_248-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks1993350-248">[221]</a></sup> This is why, according to Hegel, art can only be one of three mutually complementary modes of absolute spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-249"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-249">[ab]</a></sup>

## Christianity\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Christianity")\]

Although his understanding of Christianity evolved over time, Hegel identified as a Lutheran his entire life. One constant was his profound appreciation for the Christian insight into the intrinsic worth and freedom of every individual.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFritzman201423_250-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFritzman201423-250">[222]</a></sup>

### Early Romantic writings\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Early Romantic writings")\]

Hegel's earliest writings on Christianity date between 1783 and 1800. He was still working out his ideas at this time, and everything from this period was abandoned as fragments or unfinished drafts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1971pp._v–viii,_translator's_Prefatory_Note_251-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1971pp._v%E2%80%93viii,_translator's_Prefatory_Note-251">[223]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-252"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-252">[ac]</a></sup> Hegel was very much dissatisfied with the [dogmatism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dogmatism "Dogmatism") and positivity<sup id="cite_ref-254"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-254">[ad]</a></sup> of the Christian religion, to which he opposed the spontaneous religion of the Greeks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKroner19717_255-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKroner19717-255">[225]</a></sup> In _The Spirit of Christianity_, he proposes a sort of resolution by aligning the universality of Kantian moral philosophy with the universality of the teachings of Jesus; in paraphrase: "The moral principle of the Gospel is charity, or love, and love is the beauty of the heart, a spiritual beauty which combines the Greek Soul and Kant's Moral Reason."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKroner19719_256-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKroner19719-256">[226]</a></sup> Although he did not return to this Romantic formulation, the unification of Greek and Christian thought would remain a preoccupation throughout his life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris199327–31_257-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris199327%E2%80%9331-257">[227]</a></sup>

### _The Phenomenology of Spirit_\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: The Phenomenology of Spirit")\]

Religion is a major theme throughout the 1807 _Phenomenology of Spirit_ well before it emerges as the explicit topic of the penultimate Religion chapter.<sup id="cite_ref-258"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-258">[ae]</a></sup> This is seen most directly in the metaphysical "unhappiness" of the [Augustinian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augustinianism "Augustinianism") consciousness in chapter IV and in Hegel's depiction of the struggle of the [Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catholic_Church "Catholic Church") of the Faithful with [Enlightenment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment "Age of Enlightenment") [philosophes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophes "Philosophes") in chapter VI.<sup id="cite_ref-259"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-259">[af]</a></sup>

Hegel's proper account of Christianity, however, is to be found in the final section of the _Phenomenology_ just prior to the closing chapter, Absolute Knowing. It is presented under the heading The Revelatory Religion \[_die offenbare Religion_\]. By means of philosophical exposition of Christian doctrines such as Incarnation and Resurrection, Hegel claims to demonstrate or to make "manifest" the conceptual truth of Christianity, and so to overcome what has only been positively revealed \[_geöffenbarte_\] by explication of its underlying, revelatory truth.<sup id="cite_ref-261"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-261">[ag]</a></sup>

The heart of Hegel's interpretation of Christianity can be seen in his interpretation of the [Trinity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trinity "Trinity"). God the Father must give Himself existence as finitely human Son, the death of whom discloses His essential being as Spirit – and, crucially, according to Hegel, his \[Hegel's\] own philosophical _concept_ of spirit makes transparent what is only obscurely _represented_ in the Christian concept of the Trinity. And so it makes manifest the philosophical _truth_ of religion, which now is _known_.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1997v.2,_chapter_12_262-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1997v.2,_chapter_12-262">[229]</a></sup>

In an essay on the _Phenomenology_, George di Giovanni contrasts Kant's rational faith<sup id="cite_ref-264"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-264">[ah]</a></sup> with Hegel's rational religion. On his view, the modern role of religion consists in "expressing and nurturing spirit in its most individual forms" rather than in explaining reality. There is no longer any place for faith in opposition to knowledge. Instead, faith assumes such forms as the trust placed "in individuals close to us, or in the time and place in which we happen to live."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEdi_Giovanni2003383_265-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEdi_Giovanni2003383-265">[231]</a></sup>

In other words, according to Hegel's philosophical interpretation, Christianity does not require faith in any doctrine that is not fully justified by reason. What is left, then, is the religious community, free to minister to individual needs and to celebrate the absolute freedom of spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEdi_Giovanni2003_266-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEdi_Giovanni2003-266">[232]</a></sup>

### The Berlin lectures\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: The Berlin lectures")\]

Hegel's _Encyclopedia_ includes a section on the Revealed Religion, but it is quite short. It is his Berlin [Lectures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lectures_on_the_Philosophy_of_Religion "Lectures on the Philosophy of Religion") that contain his next presentation of Christianity, which he variously refers to as the "consummate," "absolute," or "revelatory" religion (all equivalent terms in this context).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodgson19853–4_267-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodgson19853%E2%80%934-267">[233]</a></sup> Transcripts of three of Hegel's four courses have been preserved, and they show him to be continually adjusting his emphases and exposition.<sup id="cite_ref-268"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-268">[ai]</a></sup> The interpretation of Christianity that he advances, however, is still very much that which he presented in the _Phenomenology_ – only now he is able to expound at greater length and with greater clarity upon what he had covered earlier in such a condensed fashion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinkard2000576_269-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinkard2000576-269">[234]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005139_270-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005139-270">[235]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-271"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-271">[aj]</a></sup>

### Issues of interpretation\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Issues of interpretation")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._%28Werkst.%29_-_Portr%C3%A4t_des_Martin_Luther_%28Lutherhaus_Wittenberg%29.jpg/130px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._%28Werkst.%29_-_Portr%C3%A4t_des_Martin_Luther_%28Lutherhaus_Wittenberg%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._(Werkst.)_-_Portr%C3%A4t_des_Martin_Luther_(Lutherhaus_Wittenberg).jpg)

[Martin Luther](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Luther "Martin Luther") (1483–1546), who would not likely have recognized Hegel's claim to share his theology

[Walter Jaeschke](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Jaeschke "Walter Jaeschke") questions whether [Luther](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Luther "Martin Luther") would have recognized Hegel's claim to [Protestantism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protestantism "Protestantism").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaeschke1993461–78_272-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaeschke1993461%E2%80%9378-272">[236]</a></sup> Hegel embraces the doctrine of the [priesthood of all believers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Priesthood_of_all_believers "Priesthood of all believers") with his concept of spirit, but rejects the core Lutheran doctrines of _[sola gratia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sola_gratia "Sola gratia")_ and _[sola scriptura](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sola_scriptura "Sola scriptura")_. Instead, he affirms as the "fundamental principle" of Protestantism "the obstinacy that does honor to mankind, to refuse to recognize in conviction anything not ratified by thought."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1991a22_273-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1991a22-273">[237]</a></sup> On similar grounds, [Frederick Beiser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frederick_Beiser "Frederick Beiser"), while acknowledging Hegel's apparently sincere profession of Lutheranism, describes Hegel's theology as effectively "the very opposite of Luther's."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005145–46_274-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005145%E2%80%9346-274">[238]</a></sup>

Discussing the "Hegel Renaissance" in late 20th-century Anglo-American philosophy, Beiser expresses surprise – given today's highly secular academic culture – at such a surge of interest in Hegel. For, according to Hegel, the divine is the centerpoint of philosophy. Hegel's concept of God differs from [theistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theistic "Theistic") conceptions found in orthodox Christianity and from [deistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deistic "Deistic") conceptions suggested by eighteenth-century philosophers. Nonetheless, Hegel conceptualizes God as the infinite or absolute, in agreement with the classical definition given by [St. Anselm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/St._Anselm "St. Anselm") as "that of which nothing greater can be conceived."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser20085_275-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser20085-275">[239]</a></sup>

Just how to most properly characterize Hegel's distinctive articulation of Christianity was a matter of intense debate even in his own life and, among his students, after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinkard2000661–64_276-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinkard2000661%E2%80%9364-276">[240]</a></sup> So it is likely to remain. Neither theistic, nor deistic, Hegel's god can only be articulated in the philosophical terms of the concept of spirit or his own distinctive logical vocabulary. Nevertheless, Hegel everywhere insists that his is the Christian God.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodgson2008230–52_277-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodgson2008230%E2%80%9352-277">[241]</a></sup>

## Philosophy of history\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: Philosophy of history")\]

"History," Frederick Beiser writes, "is central to Hegel's conception of philosophy." Philosophy is only possible "if it is historical, only if the philosopher is aware of the origins, context, and development of his doctrines." In this 1993 essay, titled "Hegel's Historicism," Beiser declares this to be "nothing less than a revolution in the history of philosophy."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser1993b270_278-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser1993b270-278">[242]</a></sup> In a 2011 monograph, however, Beiser excludes Hegel from his treatment of the German historicist tradition for the reason that Hegel is more interested in the philosophy of history than in the epistemological project of justifying its status as a science.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser20119_279-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser20119-279">[243]</a></sup> Moreover, against the relativistic implications of [historicism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historicism "Historicism") narrowly construed, Hegel's metaphysics of spirit supplies a _telos_, internal to history itself, in terms of which progress can be measured and assessed. This is the self-consciousness of freedom. The more that awareness of this essential freedom of spirit permeates a culture, the more advanced Hegel claims it to be.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser1993b279,_289_280-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser1993b279,_289-280">[244]</a></sup>

Because freedom, according to Hegel, is the essence of spirit, the developing self-awareness of this is just as much a development in truth as it is in political life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulgate20053,_17–21_281-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulgate20053,_17%E2%80%9321-281">[245]</a></sup> Thinking presupposes an "instinctive belief" in truth, and the history of philosophy, as recounted by Hegel, is a progressive sequence of "system-identifying" concepts of truth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis20059-10_282-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis20059-10-282">[246]</a></sup>

Whether or not Hegel is a historicist simply depends upon how one defines the term. The importance of history in Hegel's philosophy, however, cannot be denied.

German has two words for "history," _Historie_ and _Geschichte_. The first refers to "the narrative organization of empirical material." The second "includes an account of the underlying developmental logic (the 'intrinsic ground') of deeds and events." Only the latter procedure can supply a properly universal or philosophical history, and this is the procedure Hegel adopts in all of his historical writings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis2010215_283-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis2010215-283">[247]</a></sup> According to Hegel, humans are distinctly historical creatures because, not only do they exist in time, they also internalize temporal events so that they become, in a profound sense, part of what and who people are, "integral to humanity's self-understanding and self-knowledge."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis2010214_284-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis2010214-284">[248]</a></sup> This is why the history of philosophy, for instance, is integral to philosophy itself, it being literally impossible for early philosophers to think what later philosophers, afforded all the riches of their predecessors, could think – and perhaps, with this distance, work through more thoroughly or consistently.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFritzman20143_285-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFritzman20143-285">[249]</a></sup> From a later perspective, for instance, it becomes apparent that the concept of personhood includes the implication of universality such as renders contradictory any interpretation or implementation that extends it to some people, but not to others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis20059_286-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis20059-286">[250]</a></sup>

In the Introduction to his _[Lectures on the Philosophy of World History](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lectures_on_the_Philosophy_of_History "Lectures on the Philosophy of History")_, simplifying his own account, Hegel divides human history into three epochs. In what he calls the "Oriental" world, _one_ person (the pharaoh or emperor) was free. In the Greco-Roman world, _some_ people (moneyed citizens) were free. In the "Germanic" world (that is, European Christendom) _all_ persons are free.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFritzman2014120_287-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFritzman2014120-287">[251]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1975b54_288-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1975b54-288">[252]</a></sup>

In his discussion of the ancient world, Hegel provides a heavily qualified defense of slavery. As he puts it elsewhere, "slavery occurs in a transitional phase between the natural human existence and the truly ethical condition; it occurs in a world where a wrong is still right. Here, the wrong _is valid_, so that the position it occupies is a necessary one."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1991a§57A_289-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1991a%C2%A757A-289">[253]</a></sup> Hegel is clear, however, that there is an unconditional moral demand to reject the institution of slavery, and that slavery is incompatible with the rational state and the essential freedom of every individual.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulgate2005187–88_290-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulgate2005187%E2%80%9388-290">[254]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mctag_291-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-mctag-291">[255]</a></sup>

Some commentators – most notably, [Alexandre Kojève](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexandre_Koj%C3%A8ve "Alexandre Kojève") and [Francis Fukuyama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francis_Fukuyama "Francis Fukuyama") – have understood Hegel to claim that, having achieved a fully universal concept of freedom, history is complete, that it has reached its conclusion. Against this, however, it can be objected that freedom may yet be expanded in terms both of its _scope_ and its _content_. Since Hegel's day, the scope of the concept of freedom has been expanded to acknowledge the rightful inclusion of women, formerly enslaved or colonized peoples, the mentally ill, and those who do not conform to conservative norms with respect to sexual preference or gender identity, among others. As to the content of freedom, the United Nations' [International Bill of Human Rights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Bill_of_Human_Rights "International Bill of Human Rights"), just for instance, expands the concept of freedom beyond what Hegel himself articulates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFritzman2014122–23_292-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFritzman2014122%E2%80%9323-292">[256]</a></sup> Additionally, although Hegel consistently presents his philosophical histories as East-to-West narratives, scholars such as J. M. Fritzman argue that, not only is this prejudice quite incidental to the substance of Hegel's philosophical position, but that – with India now the world's largest democracy, for instance, or with South Africa's mighty efforts to transcend [apartheid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apartheid "Apartheid") – the movement of freedom back to the East may already have begun.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFritzman2014126_293-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFritzman2014126-293">[257]</a></sup>

## Dialectics, speculation, idealism\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: Dialectics, speculation, idealism")\]

Hegel is often credited with proceeding according to a "[dialectical method](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dialectical_method "Dialectical method")"; in point of fact, however, Hegel characterizes his philosophy as "speculative" (_spekulativ_), rather than dialectical, and uses the term "dialectical" only "quite rarely".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStern200215_294-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStern200215-294">[258]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-296"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-296">[ak]</a></sup> This is because, although "_Dialektik_ sometimes stands for the entire movement of the self-articulation of meaning or thought, this term refers more specifically to the self-negation of the determinations of the understanding (_Verstand_), when they are thought through in their fixedness and opposition."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1991bp._349,_editors'_n.13_297-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1991bp._349,_editors'_n.13-297">[260]</a></sup>

By contrast, "Hegel describes correct thinking as the methodical interplay of three moments\[:\]

(a) abstract and intellectual (_verständig_),

(b) dialectical or negatively rational (_negativvernünftig_), and

(c) speculative or positively rational (_positivvernünftig_)."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeperzak200157_298-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeperzak200157-298">[261]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005167–69_299-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005167%E2%80%9369-299">[262]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel1991b§§80–82_300-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel1991b%C2%A7%C2%A780%E2%80%9382-300">[263]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-303"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-303">[al]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-305"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-305">[am]</a></sup>

For example, self-consciousness is "the concept that consciousness has of itself. Thus in this case concept and referent coincide:... 'self-consciousness' refers to mind's taking on the self-contradictory (and thus also self-negating) role of being subject and object of one and the same act of cognition – simultaneously and in the same respect."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis200522_306-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Laurentiis200522-306">[267]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel2018¶166_307-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel2018%C2%B6166-307">[268]</a></sup> Hence it is a speculative concept.

According to Beiser, "if Hegel has any methodology at all, it appears to be an anti-methodology, a method to suspend all methods." Hegel's term "dialectic" must be understood with reference to the _concept_ of the object of investigation. What must be grasped is "the 'self-organization' of the subject matter, its 'inner necessity' and 'inherent movement.'" Hegel renounces all external methods such as could be "applied" to some subject matter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005160_103-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser2005160-103">[96]</a></sup>

The dialectical character of Hegel's speculative procedure often makes his position on any given issue quite difficult to characterize. Instead of seeking to answer a question or solve a problem directly, he frequently recasts it by showing, for instance, "how the dichotomy underlying the dispute is false, and that it is therefore possible to integrate elements from both positions."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStern2002xiii_308-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStern2002xiii-308">[269]</a></sup> Speculative thought preserves what is true from apparently opposing theories in a process that Hegel terms "[sublation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aufheben "Aufheben")".

To "sublate" (_aufheben_) has three main senses:<sup id="cite_ref-311"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-311">[an]</a></sup>

-   'to raise, to hold, lift up';
-   'to annul, abolish, destroy, cancel, suspend'; and,
-   'to keep, save, preserve.'<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInwood1992283_312-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInwood1992283-312">[272]</a></sup>

Hegel generally uses the term in all three senses, with particular emphasis on the second and third, in which apparent contradictions are speculatively overcome.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInwood1992283_312-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInwood1992283-312">[272]</a></sup> His word for what is sublated is "moment" (_das Moment_, in the neuter), which denotes "an essential feature or aspect of a whole conceived as a static system, and an essential phase in a whole conceived as a dialectical movement or process."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInwood1992311_313-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInwood1992311-313">[273]</a></sup> (When Hegel describes something as "contradictory," what he means is that it is not independently self-sustaining on its own terms, and so it can only be comprehended \[_begreifen_\] as a moment of a larger whole.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInwood199264_314-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInwood199264-314">[274]</a></sup>)

According to Hegel, to think of the finite as a moment of the whole, rather than an independently self-determined existent, is what it means to grasp it as idealized (_das Ideelle_).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInwood1992128–31_315-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInwood1992128%E2%80%9331-315">[275]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel2010b119_316-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel2010b119-316">[276]</a></sup> Idealism, then, "is the doctrine that finite entities are ideal (_ideell_): they depend not on themselves for their existence but on some larger self-sustaining entity \[i.e., the whole\] that underlies or embraces them."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInwood1992129_317-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInwood1992129-317">[277]</a></sup>

The pronoun-expressions – moment, sublate, and idealize – are characteristic of Hegel's account of idealism. They can be understood as stages of thought in which the "object is conceptually present first in mere adumbration, then according to circumstances both internal and external to it, and finally standing completely on its own."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEdi_Giovanni2013253_318-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEdi_Giovanni2013253-318">[278]</a></sup> This phenomenological and conceptual analysis distinguishes Hegel's idealism from Kant's [transcendental idealism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcendental_idealism "Transcendental idealism") and [Berkeley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Berkeley "George Berkeley")'s mentalistic idealism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStern2008_319-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStern2008-319">[279]</a></sup> In contrast to those positions, Hegel's idealism is entirely compatible with [realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophical_realism "Philosophical realism") and non-mechanistic [naturalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy) "Naturalism (philosophy)").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser200568–69_320-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser200568%E2%80%9369-320">[280]</a></sup> This position rejects empiricism as an a priori account of knowledge, but it is in no way opposed to the philosophical legitimacy of empirical knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPippin20195_321-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPippin20195-321">[281]</a></sup> Hegel's idealistic contention, which he claims to demonstrate, is that _being itself_ is rational.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulgate2005106_322-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulgate2005106-322">[282]</a></sup>

Although it is not incorrect to refer to Hegel's philosophy as "absolute idealism," this moniker was at the time more associated with Schelling, and Hegel himself is documented as employing it with reference to his own philosophy only three times.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeiser200558_323-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeiser200558-323">[283]</a></sup>

According to Hegel, "every philosophy is essentially idealism."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegel2010b124_324-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegel2010b124-324">[284]</a></sup> This claim is based on the assumption, which Hegel claims to demonstrate, that conceptualization is present at all cognitive levels. For to completely deny this would undermine trust in the conceptual capacities necessary for objective knowledge – and so would lead to total skepticism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStern2008170–71_325-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStern2008170%E2%80%9371-325">[285]</a></sup> Hence, according to [Robert Stern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Stern_(philosopher) "Robert Stern (philosopher)"), Hegel's idealism, "amounts to a form of _conceptual realism_, understood as 'the belief that concepts are part of the structure of reality.'"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStern2008172_326-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStern2008172-326">[286]</a></sup>

### Thesis–antithesis–synthesis\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Thesis–antithesis–synthesis")\]

This terminology, largely developed earlier by [Fichte](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte "Johann Gottlieb Fichte"), was spread by [Heinrich Moritz Chalybäus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Moritz_Chalyb%C3%A4us "Heinrich Moritz Chalybäus") in accounts of Hegel's philosophy that have since been broadly discredited.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMueller1958_327-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMueller1958-327">[287]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood19903–4_328-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood19903%E2%80%934-328">[288]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChalybäus1860367_329-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChalyb%C3%A4us1860367-329">[289]</a></sup> [Walter Kaufmann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Kaufmann_(philosopher) "Walter Kaufmann (philosopher)"), for instance, reports:

> Fichte introduced into German philosophy the three-step of thesis, antithesis, and synthesis, using these three terms. Schelling took up this terminology. _Hegel did not._ He never once used these three terms together to designate three stages in an argument or account in any of his books. And they do not help us understand his _Phenomenology_, his _Logic_, or his philosophy of history; they impede any open-minded comprehension of what he does by forcing it into a scheme which was available to him and which he deliberately spurned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufmann1959154_330-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufmann1959154-330">[290]</a></sup>

More modestly, it has been said that this account is "only a partial comprehension that requires correction." What it gets right is that, according to Hegel, "truth emerges from error" in the course of historical development in a way that implies a "holism in which partial truths are progressively corrected so that their one-sidedness is overcome." What it distorts is that such a description is possible only after the process has unfolded. The "thesis" and "antithesis" are not "alien" to one another. Inasmuch as there can be said to be such a "dialectical method," it is not an external one such as could be "applied" to some subject matter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFritzman20143–4_331-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFritzman20143%E2%80%934-331">[291]</a></sup>

Similarly, Stephen Houlgate argues that, in whatever limited sense Hegel might be said to have a "method," it is a strictly _immanent_ method; that is, it emerges from thoughtful immersion in the subject-matter itself. If this leads to dialectics, that is only because there is a contradiction in the object itself, not because of any external methodological procedure.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulgate2006ch._2_332-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulgate2006ch._2-332">[292]</a></sup>

## Reception\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: Reception")\]

Hegel's influence on subsequent philosophical developments has been enormous. In late nineteenth- and early twentieth-century England, a school known as [British idealism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_idealism "British idealism") propounded a version of [absolute idealism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_idealism "Absolute idealism") in direct engagement with Hegel's texts. Prominent members included [J. M. E. McTaggart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._M._E._McTaggart "J. M. E. McTaggart"), [R. G. Collingwood](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/R._G._Collingwood "R. G. Collingwood"), and [G. R. G. Mure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_Reginald_Gilchrist_Mure "Geoffrey Reginald Gilchrist Mure"). Separately, some philosophers such as [Marx](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marx "Marx"), [Dewey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Dewey "John Dewey"), [Derrida](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derrida "Derrida"), [Adorno](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theodor_W._Adorno "Theodor W. Adorno"), and [Gadamer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gadamer "Gadamer") have selectively developed Hegelian ideas into their own philosophical programs. Others have developed their positions in opposition to Hegel's system. These include, for instance, such diverse philosophers as [Schopenhauer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schopenhauer "Schopenhauer"), [Kierkegaard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kierkegaard "Kierkegaard"), [Russell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bertrand_Russell "Bertrand Russell"), [G. E. Moore](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G._E._Moore "G. E. Moore"), and [Foucault](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michel_Foucault "Michel Foucault"). In theology, Hegel's influence marks the work of [Karl Barth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Barth "Karl Barth") and [Dietrich Bonhoeffer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dietrich_Bonhoeffer "Dietrich Bonhoeffer"). These names, however, constitute only a small sample of some of the more important figures who have developed their thought in engagement with the philosophy of Hegel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulgate20051–2_333-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulgate20051%E2%80%932-333">[293]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFritzman2014chapter_9_334-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFritzman2014chapter_9-334">[294]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERockmore1993chapter_3_335-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTERockmore1993chapter_3-335">[295]</a></sup>

### "Right" vs. "Left" Hegelianism\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: "Right" vs. "Left" Hegelianism")\]

Some historians present Hegel's early influence in Germanic philosophy as divided into two opposing camps, right and left.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFackenheim1967ch._4,_§§2–3_336-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFackenheim1967ch._4,_%C2%A7%C2%A72%E2%80%933-336">[296]</a></sup> The [Right Hegelians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right_Hegelians "Right Hegelians"), the allegedly direct disciples of Hegel at the [Friedrich-Wilhelms-Universität](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humboldt_University_of_Berlin "Humboldt University of Berlin"), advocated a [Protestant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protestant "Protestant") orthodoxy and the [political conservatism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_conservatism "Political conservatism") of the post-[Napoleon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France "Napoleon I of France") Restoration period. The Left Hegelians, also known as the [Young Hegelians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Young_Hegelians "Young Hegelians"), interpreted Hegel in a [revolutionary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revolutionary "Revolutionary") sense, leading to an advocation of [atheism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atheism "Atheism") in religion and [liberal democracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liberal_democracy "Liberal democracy") in politics. Recent studies, however, have questioned this paradigm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELöwith1964_337-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEL%C3%B6with1964-337">[297]</a></sup>

The Right Hegelians "were quickly forgotten" and "today mainly known only to specialists"; the Left Hegelians, by contrast, "included some of the most important thinkers of the period," and "through their emphasis on practice, some of these thinkers have remained exceedingly influential," primarily through the Marxist tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERockmore2013305_338-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel#cite_note-FOOTNOTERockmore2013305-338">[298]</a></sup>

#### Marxism\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: Marxism")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Karl_Marx_001.jpg/180px-Karl_Marx_001.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Karl_Marx_001.jpg)

[Karl Marx](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx "Karl Marx") (1818–1883)

Among the first to take a critical view of Hegel's system was the 19th-century German group known as the [Young Hegelians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Young_Hegelians "Young Hegelians"), which included [Feuerbach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feuerbach "Feuerbach"), [Marx](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marx "Marx"), [Engels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Engels "Engels"), and their followers.
